Because vocal tract length is correlated with body height, formant frequencies are fundamentally relative measures, leading to descriptions based on average proportional differences and perceptual goals rather than fixed $\text{Hz}$ boundaries for all speakers.

***

### Female/Feminine Resonance

Feminine-perceived voices are characterized by higher formant frequencies, reflecting a shorter overall vocal tract and specific articulatory practices.

**Acoustic Targets (Relative Values):**

*   **Higher Frequencies:** Average formant frequency values for **cisgender females are approximately 20% higher** than those of cisgender males.
*   **Minimum Shift for Perception:** To be reliably perceived as "not-male" (female or gender neutral), achieving a **minimum increase of 20% of all relevant formant frequencies** has been shown to result in a statistically significant increase in feminine perception. For a single formant frequency to be reliably perceived as "not-male," its frequency may need to be raised to **80%–100% of cisgender female formant frequency averages**.
*   **Key Formant:** The **Second Formant (F2)**, which is influenced by the length of the oral cavity and tongue positioning, is considered highly salient in gender perception, often correlating most strongly with the perception of gender. Increasing F2 results in the perception of a feminine voice.
*   **F1 and Vowels:** The **First Formant (F1)** is most responsive to mouth opening and tongue height. Vowels that naturally have a high $\text{F}1$ (such as /ɑ/ and /æ/) are perceived as more feminine than vowels with low $\text{F}1$ (such as /i/ and /u/).

**Perceptual Characteristics:**

*   **Tone and Quality:** Feminine resonance is often described as having **brightness** in voice quality, being **forward-feeling**, and having an overall **"light"** tone.
*   **Acoustic Amplification:** Feminine-perceived resonance is associated with the **amplification of high frequencies**.
*   **Resonance Focus:** The tone is often focused in the **face or head**.
*   **Articulation/Vocal Tract Configuration:** Higher formant frequencies are achieved behaviorally through tongue and lip adjustments, such as **increased lip spreading** and a **high and forward tongue position**.

***

### Male/Masculine Resonance

Masculine-perceived voices are characterized by lower formant frequencies, reflecting a longer vocal tract and specific articulatory practices.

**Acoustic Targets (Relative Values):**

*   **Lower Frequencies:** Average formant frequency values for cisgender males are approximately **20% lower** than those of cisgender females.
*   **F1 as a Predictor:** In connected speech among trans men, the **First Formant (F1)** was found to be an acoustic predictor of vocal masculinity; specifically, for every 1 $\text{Hz}$ increase in $\text{F}1$, the probability of being recognized as masculine decreased by $1.4\%$. Voices perceived as masculine had lower average $\text{F}1$ values (e.g., $518.437 \text{ Hz}$) than voices perceived as feminine (e.g., $705.157 \text{ Hz}$).

**Perceptual Characteristics:**

*   **Tone and Quality:** Masculine resonance is described as having **darkness or brassiness** in voice quality, being **richer** or **deeper**.
*   **Acoustic Amplification:** Associated with **amplified low frequencies**.
*   **Resonance Focus:** The resonance is felt in the **throat or chest**.
*   **Vocal Tract Configuration:** Aiming for a "bigger," "darker," or richer tone may involve increasing **open oral resonance** by developing "open mouth" (dropping the jaw) and "open throat" (lowering the base of the tongue and raising the soft palate) techniques.

***

### Androgynous/Gender-Neutral Resonance

The sources do not define a specific $\text{Hz}$ range for formant frequencies that universally guarantees a neutral perception, as perception often relies on a combination of cues.

**Acoustic and Perceptual Characteristics:**

*   **Resonance Ambiguity:** When the $\text{F}_0$ falls within the **gender-ambiguous or gender-neutral range ($\text{150 Hz} \text{ - } \text{185 Hz}$)**, formant frequencies become highly salient. If $\text{F}_0$ is ambiguous but the formant frequencies are still in the typical male area, the voice is **nevertheless often perceived as that of cisgender men**. This highlights that resonance cues must align with gender goals, especially when pitch is unclear.
*   **Non-Binary Indexation:** For some listeners, especially gender-expansive listeners, a non-binary presentation may be indexed by a unique combination of cues, such as **intermediate $\text{F}_0$ values** combined with a **bright, more resonant voice quality**. This bright quality is quantified by a **less negative spectral slope** and **higher cepstral peak prominence-smoothed (CPPS)**.
*   **Therapy Goal:** For individuals seeking a gender-neutral voice, the aim is often to **modify resonance alongside pitch** to achieve a neutral quality, typically by **reducing a brighter, oralized tone**.

This request for specific values for **vocal resonance** (formant frequencies, FFs) is critical for building an effective AI voice application, as resonance is the second most salient acoustic cue for perceived gender after fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$).

Because formant frequencies are fundamentally related to the physical dimensions of the vocal tract, absolute Hz values vary by individual height and anatomy. Therefore, the most practical approach for an AI application is to use **proportional shifts** relative to a speaker's baseline male-range FFs, along with target FFs derived from normative studies on cisgender speakers.

Here is a breakdown of specific resonance targets, incorporating required proportional shifts and absolute ranges, structured for AI implementation:

---

## 1. Female/Feminine Resonance Targets

Feminine resonance is acoustically achieved by increasing formant frequencies, which gives the perception of a smaller vocal tract (e.g., shorter and narrower oral cavity).

### A. Required Proportional Shift

The primary goal for feminizing resonance is a vertical shift in the entire formant structure:

*   **Overall Increase:** Average formant frequency values for cisgender females are approximately **20% higher** than those of cisgender males.
*   **Perceptual Threshold:** To reliably be perceived as **"not-male"** (female or gender neutral), a minimum increase of **20% of all relevant formant frequencies** has been suggested.
*   **Maximum Shift for Specific Vowels:** For a single formant frequency to be perceived as "not-male" by most listeners, its frequency needed to be raised to **80%–100% of cisgender female formant frequency averages**.
*   **Targeted Articulation:** This shift is achieved behaviorally by instructing users to use articulatory modifications such as **increased lip spreading** and a **high and forward tongue position**.

### B. Salient Formant Frequencies (F1 and F2)

The most critical formants for feminine perception are the lower ones, which carry the most information about vowel identity and perceived gender:

*   **Second Formant (F2):** F2 correlates most saliently with the perception of gender. Raising F2 values is positively associated with higher perceived femininity ratings.
*   **First Formant (F1):** F1 is also considered highly salient, particularly for certain vowels. Increasing F1 generally corresponds to a more feminine perception.
    *   **Vowel Selection:** Focusing exercises on vowels with **naturally high F1 frequencies** (such as **/ɑ/** and **/æ/**) has been shown to be most effective in shifting listener perception toward "not-male" when formants are manipulated.

### C. Normative Hard Targets (Example Vowels - Hz)

The following table provides target values derived from acoustic norms for American English vowels, serving as potential targets for advanced users or as a basis for calculating the required $\text{Hz}$ shift from a speaker's masculine baseline:

| Vowel | Formant | Cis Male Average (Hz) | Cis Female Average (Hz) | Target Goal (Relative) |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: |
| **I** (/i/) | F1 | 342 | **437** | Increase FFs by $\sim 20\%$ |
| | F2 | 2322 | **2761** | |
| | F3 | 3000 | **3372** | |
| **A** (/æ/) | F1 | 588 | **669** | High F1, High F2. Highly salient. |
| | F2 | 1952 | **2349** | |
| | F3 | 2601 | **2972** | |
| **AH** (/ɑ/) | F1 | 768 | **936** | Highest F1. Most effective for perception shift. |
| | F2 | 1333 | **1551** | |
| | F3 | 2522 | **2815** | |
| **U** (/u/) | F1 | 378 | **459** | Low F1. Less effective for perception shift. |
| | F2 | 997 | **1105** | |
| | F3 | 2343 | **2735** | |

***

## 2. Male/Masculine Resonance Targets

Masculine resonance is characterized by overall lower formant frequencies, reflecting a longer vocal tract.

### A. Required Proportional Shift

*   **Overall Decrease:** Cisgender male FFs are approximately **20% lower** than those of cisgender females.
*   **F1 as Acoustic Predictor:** For trans men in connected speech, F1 was identified as an acoustic predictor of vocal masculinity.
    *   A voice perceived as **masculine** had a significantly lower F1 average, such as **518.437 Hz**.
    *   A voice perceived as **feminine** (in the trans man group) had a higher F1 average, such as **705.157 Hz**.
*   **Articulation Targets:** Masculinizing the voice typically involves altering resonance to focus on **open oral resonance** by developing an "open mouth" (dropping the jaw) and "open throat" (lowering the base of the tongue) to reduce a "brighter" tone and target lower frequencies.

### B. Importance of Alignment with Pitch

If a speaker is undergoing testosterone treatment, the vocal tract length may increase, causing a natural lowering of FFs. Regardless, if the ultimate goal is masculine attribution, the FFs must align with the $\text{F}_0$:

*   If the $\text{F}_0$ is in the ambiguous range (e.g., $150\text{ Hz} \text{ to } 185\text{ Hz}$), but the formant frequencies are still in the typical male range, the voice is often **nevertheless perceived as that of cisgender men**.

***

## 3. Androgynous/Gender-Neutral Resonance Targets

A gender-neutral voice, particularly one used by non-binary or gender-diverse individuals, relies on decoupling pitch and resonance cues, or achieving a specific combination of non-binary cues.

### A. Resonance in the Ambiguous $\text{F}_0$ Range

The concept of resonance contributing to a neutral voice is often discussed in the context where the $\text{F}_0$ falls in the ambiguous range of **$150 \text{ Hz} \text{ to } 185\text{ Hz}$**.

*   **To Maintain Ambiguity:** If the goal is truly neutral perception, the FFs should arguably fall between the normative male and female targets (Section 1.C), or avoid the typical female pattern of high F1/F2.
*   **Reducing "Feminine" Brightness:** For non-binary people seeking a neutral voice, modifying resonance is suggested to be "aimed at **reducing a brighter, oralized tone**".

### B. Specific Non-Formant Cues for Neutrality

For non-binary speakers, the perception of a neutral or non-binary voice often depends on a combination of intermediate pitch and certain non-FF voice qualities:

*   **Bright Voice Quality:** Some non-binary speakers uniquely produced the combination of **intermediate $\text{F}_0$ values** and **bright voice quality**. This quality is quantifiable:
    *   **Less Negative Spectral Slope**.
    *   **Higher Cepstral Peak Prominence-Smoothed (CPPS)**.

### C. Resonance Focus for Neutrality

If an AI is seeking a "gender-neutral" voice, it must avoid strong indexical cues:

*   **Avoid Feminine Quality:** Avoid the tonal focus (resonance) that is described as **"light"** or **"forward-feeling"**.
*   **Avoid Masculine Quality:** Avoid the tonal focus that is described as **"dark"** or felt in the **throat or chest**.
*   **Vocal Quality:** A **modal voice** quality (neutral phonation) may be neutral (without gender indexations).

***

### Summary of Other Acoustic Variables for AI Tuning

To refine the perceived gender for an app beyond FFs and $\text{F}_0$, the AI should target **Sound Pressure Level (SPL)** and **Intonation:**

| Parameter | Feminine Target | Masculine Target |
| :---: | :---: | :---: |
| **Sound Pressure Level (SPL)** (Loudness) | Lower loudness: $\mathbf{\sim 70 \text{ dBSPL}}$. Voices with greater SPL were associated with less feminine/more masculine ratings. | Higher loudness is associated with masculine presentation. |
| **Intonation/F0 Variability** | Greater variability in $\text{F}_0$, more upward and downward shifts, and a **wider $\text{F}_0$ range**. A **steep bounce** on greetings (e.g., on the telephone) is feminizing. | Less variation in $\text{F}_0$, more **flat intonation patterns**, and a **smaller $\text{F}_0$ range**. Use prosodic **"weight"** to land on important words. |
| **Articulation/Speech Rate** | More **precise, clear articulation**. Longer vowel duration. | Faster speech rate has been associated with men. |

Achieving a specific gender perception is like tuning a complex instrument; pitch sets the overall key, but resonance (the formants) determines the quality and color of the sound. If the resonance is not tuned correctly, even a high pitch will sound "like a man with a high voice".

The perception of gender based on **resonance**—which is acoustically measured by **Formant Frequencies (FFs)**—is highly complex because listeners often evaluate FFs in conjunction with Fundamental Frequency ($\text{F}_0$). While the sources provide clear ranges for $\text{F}_0$ (pitch), they describe resonance ranges primarily through **relative acoustic differences**, **vocal tract characteristics**, and **perceptual qualities** rather than fixed absolute Hertz (Hz) ranges for FFs (like $\text{F}_2$).

Here is a definition of what resonance values are associated with Masculine, Feminine, and Androgynous perception, based on the acoustic and perceptual evidence in the sources:

## I. Resonance Characteristics and Relative Values

Resonance depends on the length and shape of the vocal tract. The larger the cavities, the lower the formants; conversely, the smaller the cavities, the higher the formants.

### A. Feminine-Perceived Resonance (Higher Formants)

Feminine resonance is defined by characteristics associated with a **smaller vocal tract** and **higher formant frequencies**.

| Characteristic | Acoustic/Physiological Correlate | Key Findings |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Acoustic Value** | Formant Frequencies (FFs) are **higher**. | Cisgender women's average formant frequencies are roughly **18% higher** than cisgender men's, or **twenty percent higher** than those of cisgender men. |
| **Vocal Tract Size** | Correlates with a **smaller vocal tract**, like a violin. | Smaller female physical dimensions of the cavities of the head and neck compared to cisgender men cause the difference. |
| **Perceptual Quality** | Described as **"bright"**. | Also described as **"forward-facing"** or **"speaking into the mask"**. Associated with **amplified high frequencies**. |
| **Key Formant (F2)** | The **Second Formant ($\text{F}_2$)** is the most critical FF. | Higher $\text{F}_2$ values are associated with an increase in perceived femininity. Techniques like tongue forwarding or lip spreading increase the $\text{F}_2$ frequency. |
| **Vowel Specificity** | Vowels produced with a **more anterior articulation** (e.g., *had* and *hid* /æ/ and /ɪ/) were rated as **more feminine** than vowels with a more posterior articulation. | Overall, **front vowels** (which have higher $\text{F}_2$s) were rated as more feminine than back vowels. |
| **Training Target** | To achieve "not-male" perception, a single formant frequency needed to be raised to **80%–100% of cisgender female formant frequency averages**. It is more effective to aim at a **moderate increase of all relevant formant frequencies and vowel space**. |

### B. Masculine-Perceived Resonance (Lower Formants)

Masculine resonance is defined by characteristics associated with a **larger vocal tract**.

| Characteristic | Acoustic/Physiological Correlate | Key Findings |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Acoustic Value** | Formant Frequencies (FFs) are **lower**. | The **lower the first formant frequency ($\text{F}_1$)**, the **more masculine** a voice is judged. |
| **Vocal Tract Size** | Correlates with a **larger vocal tract**, like a cello. | This is due to anatomical differences like longer vocal tracts and lower resonant frequencies in cis men. |
| **Perceptual Quality** | Described as **"dark"**. | Also described as **"chest-focused"** or **"larger"**. Associated with **amplified low frequencies**. |

## II. Androgynous/Neutral Resonance

The concept of androgynous or neutral resonance primarily exists as a perceptual state achieved when $\text{F}_0$ and FFs fall outside the clear binary ranges, resulting in unclear gender attribution.

| Characteristic | Acoustic/Perceptual Correlate | Key Findings |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Acoustic Mismatch** | Resonance is perceived as ambiguous if $\text{F}_0$ is in the **gender ambiguous range (e.g., 150 Hz – 185 Hz)**, and the formant frequencies are **not sufficiently high** (i.e., still in the "male area"). | This mismatch results in a perceptually **"unnatural"** sound (high $\text{F}_0$ + low formants). |
| **Explicit $\text{F}_0$ Overlap Range** | While formant frequency values define resonance, the clearest quantitative definition of ambiguity comes from pitch: the androgynous, or gender neutral, Speaking Fundamental Frequency (SFF) range is typically **145 Hz to 175 Hz**. | When $\text{F}_0$ is maintained at a gender-ambiguous level (e.g., **175 Hz**), raising FFs for certain vowels can increase the perception of **"not-male"** (female or gender neutral). |
| **Non-Binary Production** | Non-binary participants produced cues combining **intermediate $\text{F}_0$ values** with **bright voice quality**. | Bright voice quality, acoustically measured by significantly less negative spectral slope, is **indicative of a brighter, more resonant voice quality**. This combination of intermediate pitch and bright resonance suggests a targeted effort to achieve a complex, non-binary, or ambiguous sonic identity. |

**In summary, the perception of resonance is relative:**

*   **Feminine:** Associated with **higher** FFs, corresponding to a **smaller vocal tract** and perceived as **"bright"** or **"forward-facing"**.
*   **Masculine:** Associated with **lower** FFs, corresponding to a **larger vocal tract** and perceived as **"dark"** or **"chesty"**.
*   **Androgynous:** A state where $\text{F}_0$ is intermediate (e.g., **150-185 Hz**) and the formants (resonance) are **inconsistent** or have been adjusted just enough to prevent the voice from being consistently perceived as male, but not enough to be consistently perceived as female.


define what the gender perceived Hz should be for male, female and adrognyous

Based on the sources, the perceived fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$ or SFF) ranges often associated with male, female, and androgynous/gender-neutral voices are described as follows:

### Fundamental Frequency ($\text{F}_0$) Ranges by Perceived Gender

The speaking fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$) is considered the most important parameter for gender perception of a voice. These ranges are derived primarily from acoustic measurements of cisgender speakers or from listener perception studies:

#### Male/Masculine Voices

Voices perceived as male or masculine generally fall into the lowest frequency range:

*   **Typical Cis Male Range:** The speaking fundamental frequency ($\text{SFF}$) for adult cisgender men is typically reported between **$107 \text{ to } 132\text{ Hz}$**, or specifically, between **$107 \text{ and } 129\text{ Hz}$** for men aged 21 to 59.
*   **Broader Male Range:** A wider range that male voices typically vary within is $\mathbf{80-165\text{ Hz}}$. Other literature reports the cis masculine voice range between $\mathbf{100 \text{ and } 150\text{ Hz}}$, $\mathbf{80 \text{ and } 150\text{ Hz}}$, and $\mathbf{110 \text{ and } 146.7\text{ Hz}}$.
*   **Perceived Masculine Mean:** Communicators consistently attributed as men had a mean $\text{F}_0$ of $\mathbf{122.93\text{ Hz}}$. Stereotypically "male" voices have a mean pitch around $\mathbf{110\text{ Hz}}$.
*   **Therapy Target:** For masculinizing a voice, the suggested average pitch range is $\mathbf{90 \text{ to } 140\text{ Hz}}$.

#### Female/Feminine Voices

Voices perceived as female or feminine occupy the highest range:

*   **Typical Cis Female Range:** The average $\text{SFF}$ for adult cisgender women generally ranges between **$189 \text{ to } 224\text{ Hz}$**, or **$188 \text{ to } 221\text{ Hz}$**.
*   **Broader Female Range:** Female voices typically vary in a range of $\mathbf{145-275\text{ Hz}}$. The expected $\text{F}_0$ for the female voice in Brazil varies between $\mathbf{150 \text{ and } 250\text{ Hz}}$.
*   **Perceived Feminine Mean:** Communicators consistently attributed as women had a mean $\text{F}_0$ of $\mathbf{194.60\text{ Hz}}$.
*   **Minimum Perception Threshold:** To be reliably perceived as female, a speaker's $\text{SFF}$ is often cited as needing to be above **$156 \text{ to } 160\text{ Hz}$**. Other required minimum average pitches cited for trans women to be perceived as female include $\mathbf{155\text{ Hz}}$, $\mathbf{165\text{ Hz}}$, $\mathbf{172\text{ Hz}}$, or $\mathbf{180\text{ Hz}}$.
*   **Therapy Targets:** A target $\text{SFF}$ for transfeminine individuals is generally to move above the cis male range ($\mathbf{100-140\text{ Hz}}$) and approach the cis female range ($\mathbf{180-220\text{ Hz}}$). A practical range for feminization exercises is recommended at $\mathbf{165-196\text{ Hz}}$.

#### Androgynous/Gender-Neutral/Ambiguous Voices

This range represents an overlap where pitch alone is insufficient for consistent gender attribution:

*   **Defined Gender Ambiguous Range:** The "gender ambiguous" or neutral range, where fundamental frequency cannot be uniquely assigned to one gender, is consistently defined as **$\mathbf{145 \text{ Hz} \text{ to } 165\text{ Hz}}$**.
*   **Extended Ambiguous Range:** A broader "gender ambiguous" or "gender neutral" range is also defined as **$\mathbf{150 \text{ to } 185\text{ Hz}}$**. This range has been suggested as a gender-acceptable pitch target for communication feminization training.
*   **Perceived Ambiguous Mean:** Communicators whose gender was attributed as **ambiguous** by raters had a mean $\text{F}_0$ of $\mathbf{173.84\text{ Hz}}$.
*   **Broad Perceived Neutral Range:** Voices perceived as gender-neutral are suggested to range between $\mathbf{\sim 131\text{ Hz}}$ (C3) and $\mathbf{\sim 262\text{ Hz}}$ (C4).

When a speaker's fundamental frequency falls within the gender ambiguous range, acoustic cues other than pitch, such as vowel formant frequencies and vocal tract resonance, become more pronounced and important for gender perception.

Based on the acoustic research regarding Speaking Fundamental Frequency ($\text{F}_0$), which is the main acoustic correlate of perceived pitch (Hz), here are the key ranges and specific values associated with male, female, and androgynous/gender-neutral voice perception.

It is critical to note that $\text{F}_0$ is only one factor; listeners use $\text{F}_0$ in conjunction with other cues like resonance (formant frequencies) and intonation. Therefore, achieving the target $\text{F}_0$ range alone may not be sufficient for consistent gender attribution.

## Gender Perceived Fundamental Frequency ($\text{F}_0$) Ranges (Hz)

### 1. Masculine-Perceived Range (Male)

This range is typically associated with the average $\text{F}_0$ of adult cisgender men, where voices are generally perceived as masculine.

| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Typical Range (Cis Men)** | **90 Hz to 140 Hz** (Approximate average range) | A heuristic threshold suggested for the masculine-perceived range. Cis male speaking range is typically approximated at **100–140 Hz**. | |
| **Broad Range (Literature)** | **80 Hz to 165 Hz** | Oates and Dacakis suggested male voices typically vary in this range. Other reports cite cis masculine voices between **80 and 150 Hz**. | |
| **Average Cis Male SFF** | **107 Hz to 132 Hz** | The average SFF for adult cisgender men. | |
| **Specific Study Data (Control)** | **110.79 Hz** (Mean SFF) | Mean SFF found in control male speaker subjects in one study. | |

### 2. Feminine-Perceived Range (Female)

This range is consistently associated with high $\text{F}_0$ values that promote the perception of female gender.

| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Typical Range (Cis Women)** | **189 Hz to 224 Hz** (Mean average range) | The average SFF levels for adult cisgender women. | |

| **Minimum Reliable Threshold** | **156 Hz to 160 Hz** | A minimum SFF value above this range is suggested as necessary for others to perceive the speaker as female. | |
| **Therapy Target Zone** | **165 Hz to 196 Hz** | Recommended range for exercises aimed at habituating a higher starting pitch. Targets suggested vary between **155 Hz and 185 Hz**, with some evidence suggesting focusing on approximately **172 Hz to 180 Hz** is a reasonable target. | |
| **Minimum Feminine Perception** | **180 Hz or higher** | Some studies report a minimum $\text{F}_0$ value of **180 Hz** required for a voice to be perceived as feminine. | |

This range is where the $\text{F}_0$ overlaps between conventionally male and female voices, making pitch ambiguous for listener attribution.

| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |

Also referred to as the **gender neutral range** for exercises or where a speaker's SFF needed to be in order to be perceived as female in early reports. | |

Mean $\text{F}_0$ was the strongest predictor of **mid-scale or "gender-neutral" assessments**. | |
| **Specific Target Value** | **175 Hz** | This $\text{F}_0$ value has been used as a target for a gender-ambiguous pitch in studies investigating the effect of formant manipulation. | |

Why is fundamental frequency critical to gender attribution?


The **fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$)**, or perceived pitch, is considered **critical** and the **most salient acoustic cue** for gender attribution because it is the acoustic variable that accounts for the greatest amount of listener variance in determining a speaker's gender.

Here is a detailed discussion on why $\text{F}_0$ is critical to gender attribution, despite not being the only factor:

### 1. $\text{F}_0$ is the Primary Predictor of Attributed Gender

$\text{F}_0$ is consistently emphasized in both transgender and cisgender literature for its critical role in gender attribution.

*   **Statistical Weight:** A meta-analysis exploring the contribution of $\text{F}_0$ to gender attribution found that this single measure accounted for **$41.6\%$ of the variance** in attributed gender.
*   **Predictive Power:** Results from a multinomial logistic regression analysis identified **mean $\text{F}_0$ as the *sole acoustic measure*** that significantly changed the odds of a speaker being attributed as a **woman or ambiguous in gender** rather than as a man. For every unit increase in mean $\text{F}_0$, the odds that an individual would be attributed as a woman or ambiguous in gender increased by a factor of 1.107 and 1.079, respectively.
*   **Listener Consistency:** In the normally phonated condition, listeners consistently use **$\text{F}_0$ to rate femininity/masculinity**. Voices with a higher $\text{F}_0$ were rated as more feminine/less masculine, and vice-versa.

### 2. Biological and Social Basis for $\text{F}_0$ Differences

The differences in $\text{F}_0$ between male and female voices are influenced by anatomical distinctions, which are often reinforced by social expectations.

*   **Anatomy:** $\text{F}_0$ differences are largely based on **anatomical differences in the size of the vocal folds and larynx**. Higher $\text{F}_0$ is produced by faster vocal fold vibrations, which result from **smaller, shorter vocal folds**, typical of cisgender women.
*   **Cultural Expectations:** Social expectations and cultural traditions influence pitch interpretation; **deeper voices are frequently associated with masculinity and higher-pitched voices are associated with femininity**.

### 3. $\text{F}_0$ Defines the Gender Thresholds

$\text{F}_0$ is used to define the ranges that listeners rely upon for categorization:

*   **Binary Ranges:** The male voice typically has an average $\text{F}_0$ range of $\mathbf{80 \text{ to } 165\text{ Hz}}$, while the female voice is typically in the range of $\mathbf{145 \text{ to } 275\text{ Hz}}$.
*   **Feminine Threshold:** A spoken $\text{F}_0$ above $\mathbf{156 \text{ to } 160\text{ Hz}}$ is often reported as **necessary** for listeners to perceive the speaker as female. The minimum threshold suggested for a transgender woman to be attributed as a woman has been cited as approximately $155 \text{ to } 160\text{ Hz}$.
*   **Ambiguous Range:** The overlap range, where $\text{F}_0$ cannot be uniquely assigned to one gender, is typically defined as **$\mathbf{145 \text{ Hz} \text{ to } 165\text{ Hz}}$** or $\mathbf{150 \text{ Hz} \text{ to } 185\text{ Hz}}$.

### 4. $\text{F}_0$'s Interaction with Other Cues

Although critical, $\text{F}_0$ is not a sufficient predictor of gender attribution, which further reinforces why it is always the focus of study: to determine the limits of its contribution.

*   **The Conflict Problem:** Studies indicate that modifying $\text{F}_0$ alone is often insufficient for consistent attribution. Even when $\text{F}_0$ is raised to the female range, the voice may still be perceived as male. Conversely, some trans women who use average $\text{F}_0$ within the normal range for women are still perceived as men.
*   **Resonance Interaction:** The role of other acoustic factors becomes **more pronounced** when $\text{F}_0$ falls within the "gender ambiguous" range. For example, when $\text{F}_0$ is held constant at an ambiguous level (e.g., $175 \text{ Hz}$), listeners' perceptions shift to "not-male" only when the **formant frequencies (resonance)** are manipulated, particularly for vowels with naturally high F1 frequencies.
*   **Combined Strength:** The maximum accuracy for gender shift is achieved when **both $\text{F}_0$ and formant frequencies are altered** to reflect the opposite gender's normative values.

In conclusion, $\text{F}_0$ is critical because it is the **primary determinant of pitch** and the **strongest single predictor** that determines whether a voice will fall into the masculine, feminine, or ambiguous perceptual space. While resonance, intonation, and vocal quality are necessary for achieving a **natural-sounding** voice that fully aligns with an affirmed gender, $\text{F}_0$ is the foundational acoustic marker that sets the perceptual stage for gender judgment.

Fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$), the acoustic measure corresponding to the perceived **pitch** of the voice, is considered **critical** to gender attribution due to its fundamental role as the **most salient acoustic cue** that influences listener perception of gender.

Here is a detailed discussion on why $\text{F}_0$ is critical to gender attribution, supported by acoustic and perceptual research:

### 1. $\text{F}_0$ is the Sole Acoustic Measure Driving Binary Attribution

Research demonstrates the unparalleled importance of $\text{F}_0$ in sorting voices into binary gender categories:

*   **Statistical Predictor:** Results of a multinomial logistic regression analysis identified **mean fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$) as the sole acoustic measure** that changed the odds of a speaker being attributed as a woman or ambiguous in gender rather than as a man.
*   **Odds Ratio:** As the mean $\text{F}_0$ increased, the chances of being attributed a gender other than man also increased. Specifically, for every unit increase in mean $\text{F}_0$, the **odds that an individual would be attributed as a woman rather than a man increased by a factor of 1.133** or, when $\text{F}_0$ alone was included as a predictor, the odds increased by a factor of 1.107.
*   **High Predictive Power:** A model including only $\text{F}_0$ correctly predicted gender attribution in **85% of cases**. Furthermore, a meta-analysis showed that $\text{F}_0$ accounted for **41.6% of the variance** in attributed gender.

### 2. Physical and Psychoacoustic Basis

The importance of $\text{F}_0$ is rooted in the biological differences between sexes and the resulting psychoacoustic perception of pitch:

*   **Anatomical Differences:** Differences in $\text{F}_0$ between male and female speakers are based on **anatomical differences in the size of the vocal folds and larynx**. Testosterone increases in males during puberty cause vocal folds to become longer and thicker, resulting in a lower $\text{F}_0$.
*   **Psychoacoustic Correlate:** $\text{F}_0$ is the primary determinant of the **perceived pitch** of the voice. Listener perception of a speaker's pitch depends mostly on the fundamental frequency in the speaker's voice.
*   **Gender Ranges:** The distinct ranges for cisgender individuals make $\text{F}_0$ an effective binary cue: the average $\text{F}_0$ for adult cisgender men is approximately **107–132 Hz** or **110–130 Hz**, while the average $\text{F}_0$ for adult cisgender women is approximately **189–224 Hz** or **180–230 Hz**. This difference can be nearly an octave.

### 3. Role in Voice Feminization Training

$\text{F}_0$ is the foundational goal for feminization therapy, necessary to shift the voice into a perceived female or ambiguous range:

*   **Therapeutic Target:** Because $\text{F}_0$ remains a **predominant parameter** in the perception of the female voice, many authors state that it **must be raised** to increase vocal femininity. This is a common and recommended practice.
*   **Feminine Threshold:** A Speaking Fundamental Frequency (SFF) **above 156–160 Hz** is necessary for others to perceive the speaker as female. Some studies suggest a minimum $\text{F}_0$ of **180 Hz** is required for a voice to be perceived as feminine.
*   **Femininity Correlation:** $\text{F}_0$ has been positively related to femininity ratings made by both unfamiliar listeners and transgender women themselves. Higher $\text{F}_0$ values are associated with more feminine/less masculine ratings. Within normally phonated speech, speakers were rated as **more feminine as mean $\text{F}_0$ increased**.

### 4. Limitations: The Need for Interaction

Despite its critical role, $\text{F}_0$ alone is not a guarantee of successful gender attribution, reinforcing the need to modify other factors.

*   **Insufficient Metric:** $\text{F}_0$ alone is often an **insufficient endpoint** to determine the success of voice training. Raising $\text{F}_0$ alone is **frequently insufficient** for changing auditory perception of vocal gender from male to female.
*   **Overlap Range:** The existence of a **gender ambiguous or neutral range** (approximately **150–185 Hz** or **145–180 Hz**) is where the influence of $\text{F}_0$ lessens, and other factors become critical. Mean $\text{F}_0$ was the **strongest predictor of mid-scale or "gender-neutral" assessments**.
*   **Required Collaboration with Resonance:** Research strongly suggests that $\text{F}_0$ and **formant frequencies (FFs)** must **both be changed in concert** with one another to achieve a reliable shift in gender perception. If $\text{F}_0$ is raised but the formant frequencies are still in the masculine range, the voice may still be perceived as male. The perception of gender is influenced by both FFs and $\text{F}_0$, acting **in conjunction with one another**. The removal of $\text{F}_0$ (such as in whispered speech) causes listener ratings to become **more ambiguous** (closer to the midline).


What percentage of gender perception variance does F0 account for?

The percentage of gender perception variance accounted for by the fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$) has been estimated based on meta-analysis and individual study results:

### Meta-Analysis Result

A systematic review and meta-analysis specifically exploring the contribution of $\text{F}_0$ to gender attribution reported that this measure accounted for **$41.6\%$ of the variance in attributed gender** or **gender perception**.

This figure suggests that while $\text{F}_0$ is the **most studied** and often considered the **most salient** cue, the majority of the variance remained unexplained by pitch alone:

*   **$58.4\%$** of the variance in gender attribution remained to be explained by other communication factors.
*   This indicates that $\text{F}_0$ alone is generally considered **insufficient** to consistently achieve a feminine perception.

### Single Study Logistic Regression

In a separate study using a multinomial logistic regression analysis to predict gender attribution, a model including **only $\text{F}_0$** was found to explain **$71.2\%$ of the variance** in gender attribution.

*   In this specific study, this result left **$28.8\%$** of the variance remaining to be explained by other variables.

Fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$) is a critical acoustic cue for gender attribution, and sources have quantified its contribution to the variance in gender perception.

Based on a systematic review and meta-analysis, the mean fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$) of the voice contributed to **41.6% of the variance in gender perception**.

Key takeaways regarding this percentage:

*   **Remaining Variance:** Although $\text{F}_0$ accounts for a significant portion of the variance, a substantial portion—**58.4%**—remained to be explained by other communication factors.
*   **Insufficiency of $\text{F}_0$ Alone:** This 41.6% figure suggests that listeners' perceptions may **not change** from male to female or masculine to feminine by altering pitch alone. It demonstrates that $\text{F}_0$ alone is an **insufficient endpoint** to determine the success of voice training.
*   **Other Factors:** The remaining variance is attributed to other aspects of communication, such as vowel formant frequencies, $\text{F}_0$ variability, minimum and maximum $\text{F}_0$, intonation measures (directional shifts and semitone range), Sound Pressure Level (SPL), and acoustic correlates of voice quality (e.g., Soft Phonation Index or SPI).
*   **Model Fit in Specific Studies:** In one study where a model included *only* $\text{F}_0$ as a predictor of gender attribution, the model explained **71.2% of the variance** in gender attribution. This model correctly predicted gender attribution in 85% of cases.


Which acoustic cue is most salient for gender attribution?

The acoustic cue that is **most salient** for gender attribution, according to the research, is the **Fundamental Frequency ($\text{F}_0$)**, which is the physical correlate of perceived pitch.

Here is a comprehensive discussion explaining why $\text{F}_0$ is the most salient cue, while also acknowledging the critical role of other factors in achieving successful gender perception:

### 1. $\text{F}_0$ as the Primary Determinant of Gender Attribution

Fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$) is widely established in both transgender and cisgender literature as playing an **important, perhaps even critical, role** in gender attribution.

*   **Sole Significant Predictor in Regression:** The results of a multinomial logistic regression analysis identified **mean fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$) as the sole acoustic measure** that significantly changed the odds of a speaker being attributed as a woman or ambiguous in gender, rather than as a man. As mean $\text{F}_0$ increased, the chances of being attributed a gender other than man also increased.
*   **High Predictive Power:** A study investigating acoustic predictors found that a model including **only $\text{F}_0$** explained **71.2% of the variance** in gender attribution, and this model **correctly predicted gender attribution in 85% of cases**.
*   **Percentage of Variance Accounted For:** A meta-analysis exploring the contribution of $\text{F}_0$ to gender attribution showed that this measure accounted for **41.6% of the variance** in attributed gender.
*   **Perceptual Correlate:** $\text{F}_0$ is the physical correlate of perceived **pitch**, and **pitch** is the first criterion that comes to mind when recognizing a voice as that of a man or a woman. In a survey of transgender people, **41.9%** chose **pitch** as the most important factor in the recognition of identified gender, followed by intonation (32.3%) and articulation (25.8%).
*   **Salience in Normally Phonated Speech:** In analyses examining speaker characteristics, listeners consistently use $\text{F}_0$ to rate femininity/masculinity in the **normally phonated condition**.

### 2. The Interaction and Limits of $\text{F}_0$

Despite its saliency, $\text{F}_0$ alone is **not sufficient** for consistent gender attribution, especially when the voice falls into the gender-ambiguous range.

*   **Remaining Variance:** While $\text{F}_0$ accounts for 41.6% of the variance in gender attribution, the remaining **58.4%** must be explained by other communication factors, such as vocal tract resonance (formant frequencies), intonation, loudness, and voice quality.
*   **Insufficient Pitch Shift:** Transgender women sometimes find that their voices continue to be perceived as male despite adopting a higher $\text{F}_0$. $\text{F}_0$ elevation is often insufficient for a voice to be recognized as female.
*   **Loss of $\text{F}_0$ Cue:** When $\text{F}_0$ is removed, such as in whispered speech, listeners rate stimuli more ambiguously (closer to the center of the scale). This finding suggests that the absence of the **most salient cue** ($\text{F}_0$) reduces confidence in gender perception.
*   **F0 vs. Resonance/Formants:** Some sources note that the psychoacoustic percept of the **resonance structure** of the voice can easily **drown out the gender percept gained from pitch**. In some perception studies using synthesized speech, $\text{F}_0$ was the most important single parameter, **followed by formant frequency (FF)**. Furthermore, shifting **both $\text{F}_0$ and formant frequencies** is considered the **most effective treatment method** in changing the perception of gender.

### 3. Other Highly Relevant Cues

Following $\text{F}_0$, vocal tract **resonance (Formant Frequencies)** is considered the second most important acoustic cue.

*   **Resonance/FFs:** Vowel formant frequencies are now generally accepted to be contributors to gender attribution. The sources suggest that resonance may sometimes influence gender perception even more than pitch.
*   **Prosody/Intonation:** Prosody, which encompasses pitch variation and stress, contributes significantly to gender perception alongside average pitch. The intonation parameters $\text{F}_0$ range and $\text{F}_0$ variation index were found to have moderate positive correlations with femininity/masculinity ratings across all sentence types.
*   **Loudness (SPL):** **Mean sound pressure level (SPL)** was identified as a predictor of masculinity–femininity ratings, alongside mean $\text{F}_0$ and average formant frequency. Higher SPL was associated with less feminine/more masculine ratings.

Based on the sources, the acoustic cue consistently identified as the **most salient** and **critical** for gender attribution is the **Fundamental Frequency ($\text{F}_0$)**, or perceived pitch.

Here is a detailed breakdown of the salience of $\text{F}_0$ and other acoustic cues:

### 1. Fundamental Frequency ($\text{F}_0$) / Pitch

$\text{F}_0$ is generally accepted as the primary determinant in listener judgments of a speaker's gender.

*   **Critical Role:** $\text{F}_0$ plays an "important, perhaps even **critical**, role in gender attribution" in both transgender and cisgender literature. It is widely agreed to be the **most salient acoustic indicator of gender** in speech.
*   **Variance Explained:** Studies show that $\text{F}_0$ accounts for a large portion of the listener's decision-making process. Results of a meta-analysis found that $\text{F}_0$ accounted for **$41.6\%$ of the variance in attributed gender**. In another logistic regression analysis, $\text{F}_0$ alone was found to explain **$71.2\%$ of the variance in gender attribution**.
*   **Statistical Predictor:** A multinomial logistic regression analysis identified **mean $\text{F}_0$ as the *sole acoustic measure*** that significantly changed the odds of a speaker being attributed as a woman or ambiguous in gender, rather than as a man.
*   **Perceptual Target:** $\text{F}_0$ is a "predominant parameter in the perception of female voice". Listener ratings show that they consistently use $\text{F}_0$ to rate femininity/masculinity in normally phonated speech.

### 2. Vocal Tract Resonance (Formant Frequencies / FFs)

Vocal tract resonance, measured by vowel formant frequencies, is the **second most salient** acoustic cue.

*   **Significant Contribution:** Resonance is considered a "vitally important" quality that **"can influence gender perception even more than pitch"**.
*   **Vowel Formants:** Vowel formant frequencies (FFs) are generally accepted to be contributors to gender attribution. The **Second Formant ($\text{F}2$)** is often highlighted as correlating **most saliently** with the perception of gender.
*   **Acoustic Predictor:** Formant frequencies were identified as significant predictors of masculinity-femininity ratings alongside $\text{F}_0$.
*   **Interaction with $\text{F}_0$:** Studies agree that the perception of gender is influenced by both $\text{F}_0$ and FFs **acting in conjunction with one another**. If $\text{F}_0$ is raised to a gender-ambiguous range ($\mathbf{150 \text{ Hz} \text{ to } 185\text{ Hz}}$), the voice may still be perceived as masculine if the formant frequencies remain in the male area. This interaction confirms that while $\text{F}_0$ sets the pitch range, FFs provide necessary corroborating information about vocal quality and size.

### 3. Other Salient Cues

While $\text{F}_0$ and formants are primary, other acoustic and prosodic features also contribute to listener perception of gender:

*   **Intonation/Prosody (Contour):** Measures related to intonation, such as $\text{F}_0$ variability and pitch contour, contribute to gender perception. For example, ascending intonation was identified as an auditory-perceptual predictor of vocal femininity in transgender women.
*   **Loudness (Sound Pressure Level / SPL):** SPL is described as one of the **most salient perceptual features of the voice**. Louder voices are generally associated with masculine gender presentations. SPL was found to be a significant predictor of masculinity-femininity ratings alongside $\text{F}_0$ and average formant frequency.
*   **Voice Quality:** Voice quality, including characteristics like **breathiness** (a feminine voice characteristic) or **roughness/croakiness** (associated with masculine voices), has been proposed as a cue for gender attribution.
*   **Articulation/Speech Rate:** Rate of speech (RoS) and articulation patterns are also considered contributors to listener perceptions of speaker gender.



What three cues predict masculinity-femininity ratings?


Based on the sources, a multiple linear regression analysis identified a specific set of three acoustic cues as significant predictors of perceived masculinity-femininity ratings in a study involving transgender and cisgender speakers.

The three acoustic cues that significantly predicted masculinity-femininity ratings were:

1.  **Mean Fundamental Frequency ($\text{F}_0$)**
2.  **Average Formant Frequency** (specifically, the average formant frequency of /i/ in one model)
3.  **Mean Sound Pressure Level (SPL)**

Together, these three variables explained a large portion of the variance in masculinity-femininity ratings (adjusted $R^2 = 0.789$).

### Contribution and Directionality

The analysis also detailed the relative contribution of these cues and the direction of their relationship with femininity ratings:

1.  **Mean $\mathbf{F}_0$:** Made the **greatest contribution** to the predictive model. Higher speaking frequencies (higher $\text{F}_0$) were positively related to the ratings, meaning they were associated with **more feminine/less masculine** ratings.
2.  **Average Formant Frequency:** Made the second greatest contribution to the model. Higher average formant frequency values were also positively related to the ratings, meaning they were associated with **more feminine/less masculine** ratings.
3.  **Mean Sound Pressure Level (SPL):** Made the smallest contribution among the three, but was a significant predictor. SPL was negatively related to the ratings, meaning voices with **greater SPL** (loudness) were associated with **less feminine/more masculine** ratings.

The sources conclude that recognizing $\text{F}_0$, average formant frequency, and SPL as predictors supports the continued targeting of these parameters in voice and communication training programs, with more emphasis potentially placed on vocal intensity (SPL).

Based on multiple linear regression analyses aimed at identifying the acoustic factors that predict perceived masculinity-femininity ratings, the three most salient cues identified in one comprehensive study were **mean fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$)**, **average formant frequency**, and **mean sound pressure level (SPL)**.

These three acoustic cues collectively explained a large majority of the variability in masculinity–femininity ratings, accounting for **78.9% of the variance** (adjusted $R^2 = 0.789$).

Here is a detailed breakdown of how each cue influences the ratings:

### 1. Mean Fundamental Frequency ($\text{F}_0$)

$\text{F}_0$ (pitch) is the most prominent predictor and is strongly associated with the gender spectrum.

*   **Direction of Influence:** $\text{F}_0$ was **positively related** to the ratings.
*   **Perceptual Correlate:** **Higher speaking frequencies** are associated with **more feminine** ratings and **less masculine** ratings.
*   **Contribution:** $\text{F}_0$ contributed the **most** to the predictive model. This finding confirms $\text{F}_0$ as an important cue to perceived masculinity–femininity and supports the goal of raising $\text{F}_0$ in training for a more feminine-sounding voice.

### 2. Average Formant Frequency (Vocal Tract Resonance)

Average formant frequency, related to vocal tract resonance, is the second strongest predictor. The study used the average of the first three formants of the vowel /i/ as a measure of resonance.

*   **Direction of Influence:** Average formant frequency was **positively related** to the ratings.
*   **Perceptual Correlate:** **Higher vocal tract resonance characteristics** (higher formants) are associated with **more feminine** ratings and **less masculine** ratings.
*   **Saliency:** This acoustic measure is considered a **salient cue** for conveying one's masculinity or femininity to others. Vocal tract resonance is generally accepted to be a contributor to gender attribution and is a common training goal.

### 3. Mean Sound Pressure Level (SPL)

SPL, or vocal loudness, is the third significant predictor identified in the multiple linear regression analysis.

*   **Direction of Influence:** SPL was **negatively related** to the ratings.
*   **Perceptual Correlate:** **Higher SPL values** (greater loudness) were associated with **less feminine** ratings and **more masculine** ratings.
*   **Clinical Implication:** The identification of SPL as a significant predictor suggests preliminary evidence to support **more deliberate targeting of vocal loudness** in voice feminization training programs as a means of refining gender presentation. For instance, the communicator rated as most feminine spoke at $69.39 \text{ dBSPL}$.

***

In summary, the statistical model found that listeners rated voices as more feminine when they had a higher pitch ($\text{F}_0$), higher resonance (average formant frequency), and lower loudness (SPL).


Tell me more about resonance and gender perception.

Resonance is a **critical element** of voice that significantly influences the perception of gender, serving as the second most salient acoustic cue after fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$).

Resonance is often described in terms of **brightness versus darkness** or **vocal space** (smallness versus largeness of the vocal tract). It is controlled by the **size and shape of the vocal tract**, which acts as a resonating cavity that filters and modifies the pitch produced by the vocal folds.

Here is a detailed look at resonance and its relationship with gender perception:

### 1. Resonance as a Key Indicator of Gender

Resonance is considered "vitally important" because it **can influence gender perception even more than pitch**. While $\text{F}_0$ is the pitch, resonance is the resulting **color** or **quality** of that pitch.

*   **Acoustic Measurement:** Resonance is acoustically measured by **vowel formant frequencies (FFs)**, which are the concentrations of acoustic energy determined by the shape of the vocal tract.
*   **Proportional Difference:** Average formant frequency values for cisgender females are approximately **20% higher** than those of cisgender males. This difference is partly anatomical (smaller vocal tract in women) but is also significantly influenced by **behavioral choices**.
*   **Perceptual Challenge:** Although resonance has the largest impact on the perceived gender of a voice, it is **notoriously hard to measure** using simple feedback tools and cannot be reliably measured instrumentally; it must be judged subjectively. Since the human ear tends to hear **bright and dark as high and low**, it can be challenging to differentiate resonance from pitch during practice.

### 2. Resonance and Feminine Perception

Feminine-perceived resonance is associated with the perception of a smaller vocal tract and specific vocal behaviors.

*   **Acoustic Characteristics:** Feminine resonance is characterized by **higher formant frequencies**, the amplification of **high frequencies**, and a corresponding perception of **brightness** in voice quality.
*   **Perceptual Descriptions:** Feminine resonance is described as **light**, **forward-feeling**, and vibrating in the **face or head**.
*   **Salient Formants:** The **Second Formant ($\text{F}2$)** is considered highly salient, correlating most strongly with the perception of gender. Higher $\text{F}2$ values were significantly associated with an **increase in the perceived femininity** of speech.
*   **Vowel Specificity:** Vowels produced with a **more anterior articulation** (which tend to have higher $\text{F}2$ values) and vowels characterized by **naturally high F1 frequencies** (such as /ɑ/ and /æ/) are more readily perceived as "not-male" or feminine.
*   **Behavioral Modification:** Clinicians target resonance using **articulatory modifications** like **lip spreading/rounding**, **anterior tongue carriage**, and a **higher and more forward tongue position** to feminize the voice quality. This approach may involve the "/i/-ification of vowels" to brighten them.

### 3. Resonance and Masculine Perception

Masculine-perceived resonance is associated with a larger vocal tract.

*   **Acoustic Characteristics:** Masculine resonance is associated with **lower formant frequencies** and the perception of **darkness or brassiness** in voice quality.
*   **Perceptual Descriptions:** Masculine resonance is described as **heavy**, **dark**, or **chesty**.
*   **Behavioral Modification:** Masculinization often involves seeking a **depth of tone** and opening up the **lower spaces** (neck and chest) to make the tones **richer/deeper**.

### 4. Resonance and Non-Binary/Ambiguous Perception

Resonance is highly important when pitch is ambiguous, and specific resonance qualities may index non-binary gender.

*   **Resonance Dominance:** If $\text{F}_0$ is in the **gender ambiguous range** ($\mathbf{150 \text{ Hz} \text{ - } \mathbf{185 \text{ Hz}}}$), but the formant frequencies are **still in the male area**, the voice is **nevertheless often perceived as that of cisgender men**. This suggests that resonance must align with gender goals, especially when pitch is unclear.
*   **Neutral Goals:** Non-binary individuals may seek to **modify resonance alongside pitch** so that the voice is generally **more neutral**, often aimed at **reducing a brighter, oralized tone**.
*   **Unique Combination:** A majority of AFAB (assigned female at birth) non-binary participants uniquely produced a combination of **intermediate $\text{F}_0$ values** and **bright voice quality**. This **bright voice quality** is acoustically quantified by a **less negative spectral slope** and **higher cepstral peak prominence-smoothed (CPPS)**. This cue combination may index the non-binary gender category for some listeners.

### 5. Interaction with Pitch and Training Efficacy

The interplay between resonance and $\text{F}_0$ is key to gender perception.

*   **Joint Contribution:** $\text{F}_0$ and formant frequencies contribute significantly to gender perception, acting in **conjunction with one another**. Shifting both $\text{F}_0$ and FFs to target values was usually effective in altering listener perception.
*   **Formant Manipulation:** Studies confirm that manipulating resonance is effective in shifting perception:
    *   **Formant Biofeedback** helped speakers shift their $\text{F}2$ frequency, and this higher $\text{F}2$ was associated with increased perceived femininity.
    *   **Articulation Exercises** (like lip spreading) caused subtle changes in FFs (e.g., increased $\text{F}2$ /a/) and were associated with **significantly increased listener perceptions of femininity** of the voice.
    *   **Combined Training** (e.g., Articulation-Resonance Training and Pitch Elevation Training) resulted in **higher femininity scores** compared to the separate trainings.

Resonance, or the color of the voice, acts like the **tonal filter** on a photograph; while $\text{F}_0$ sets the subject (the underlying pitch), resonance applies the final stylistic quality, determining whether that sound is perceived as bright and small (feminine) or dark and large (masculine).

Resonance is a paramount aspect of voice production that plays a **critical role** in gender perception, often considered the **second most important acoustic cue** after fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$). For voice feminization, resonance is sometimes argued to be even **more important than pitch** in influencing gender perception.

Resonance, acoustically measured by **Formant Frequencies (FFs)**, relates to how sound reverberates within the vocal tract (the spaces of the throat, mouth, and nasal passages). The vocal tract acts as a filter that selectively amplifies certain frequencies (formants).

Here is a detailed discussion of resonance and its contribution to gender perception:

## 1. Acoustic Basis and Physiological Differences

Resonance characteristics are directly determined by the physical dimensions of the vocal tract.

*   **Vocal Tract Size:** The size and shape of the vocal tract filter the vibrations produced by the vocal folds. The **larger the cavities** (vocal tract length/volume), the **lower the formants**; conversely, the **smaller the cavities**, the **higher the formants**.
*   **Gender-Based Differences:** Due to anatomical differences in the size of the vocal tract, the average formant frequency values for cisgender males are significantly **lower** than those of cisgender females. Cisgender women's average formant frequencies are approximately **20% higher** than those of cisgender males. This difference is caused by the **smaller female physical dimensions** of the cavities of the head and neck compared to cisgender men.
*   **Acoustic Correlates:** **Lower formant values** correlate with a **larger vocal tract** (typically masculine), while **higher formant values** correlate with a **smaller vocal tract** (typically feminine).

## 2. Resonance and Perceived Gender Quality

Perceptually, resonance dictates the voice's **timbre** (color or tone quality). Resonance is responsible for the overall tone or timbre of the voice.

### Feminine Resonance ("Bright")

*   **Perceptual Quality:** Feminine resonance is often described qualitatively as **"bright"**, **"light"**, **"forward-feeling"**, or **"forward resonance"**. Achieving this quality is a key goal for vocal feminization.
*   **Acoustic Correlate:** This quality correlates acoustically with **higher formant frequencies**. Specifically, it correlates with a **smaller vocal tract**, sometimes likened to a violin.
*   **Location:** Feminine resonance involves placing the voice in the **head** or **nasal cavity** (forward resonance). The objective in transfeminine training is to bring the focus of the tone up into the facial mask and head, diminishing the perception of a "chestier" sound.

### Masculine Resonance ("Dark")

*   **Perceptual Quality:** Masculine resonance is often described as **"dark"** or **"darkness"**, **"chesty"**, **"chest-focused"**, or **"larger"**.
*   **Acoustic Correlate:** This quality correlates with **lower formant frequencies**, indicating a **larger vocal tract**, sometimes likened to a cello.
*   **Location:** Masculine resonance is often associated with the **chest** or **neck**. Transgender men may seek a depth of tone reverberated around the neck and chest.

## 3. The Role of Specific Formants and Vowels

Vowel formant frequencies (FFs) are crucial because they signal the identity of the vowel, but slight changes also affect the voice quality and gender perception.

*   **Average Formant Frequency:** Average formant frequency, particularly of the vowel /i/, was identified as a significant predictor of **masculinity–femininity ratings**, alongside $\text{F}_0$ and mean sound pressure level.
*   **Second Formant ($\text{F}_2$):** The **Second Formant ($\text{F}_2$)** is strongly salient for gender perception. $\text{F}_2$ is related to the size of the oral cavity and changes in its frequency are often the focus of feminization efforts. **Higher $\text{F}_2$ values** are associated with an **increase in the perceived femininity** of speech.
*   **First Formant ($\text{F}_1$):** $\text{F}_1$ is most responsive to changes in mouth opening. Voices were convincingly perceived as "not-male" for vowels characterized by a **high $\text{F}_1$ frequency**.
*   **Vowel Articulation:** Overall, **front vowels**—which have **higher $\text{F}_2$s**—were rated as **more feminine** than back vowels—which have lower $\text{F}_2$s. Specifically, the stimuli /had/ and /hid/ were rated as more feminine than /hod/ and /who'd/. Articulatory changes, such as a **higher and more forward tongue position** and increased lip spreading/smile tone, help feminize voice quality by raising FFs.

## 4. The Critical Interaction with Pitch ($\text{F}_0$)

While $\text{F}_0$ is often identified as the most salient cue, resonance plays a vital, independent, and interactive role.

*   **Resonance Drowning Out Pitch:** Some evidence suggests that the **psychoacoustic percept of the resonance structure** of the voice can easily **drown out the gender percept gained from pitch**.
*   **The Mismatch Problem:** If a speaker raises their $\text{F}_0$ (pitch) but fails to **"brighten" their resonance** (raise their formants), the resulting sound (High $\text{F}_0$ + Low Formants) can be perceptually **"unnatural"** or "mismatched". Transgender women whose $\text{F}_0$ is in the **gender ambiguous zone (150 Hz – 185 Hz)** are often perceived as cisgender men if the formant frequencies are **still in the male area**.
*   **Joint Contribution is Key:** Research overwhelmingly supports that **$\text{F}_0$ and formant frequencies (FFs) act in conjunction** with one another to influence the perceived gender of a speaker. Studies found that manipulating **both F0 and formant frequencies** was **usually effective** in changing gender perception, whereas shifting only one cue was significantly less effective.



How do formant frequencies and F0 jointly affect gender perception?

The fundamental frequency ($\text{F}_0$) and vowel formant frequencies (FFs) are the two most influential acoustic cues in determining the perceived gender of a voice, and they **jointly affect gender perception** by interacting with each other.

While $\text{F}_0$ (pitch) is generally considered the **most salient** or **critical** acoustic indicator of gender, its effectiveness in shifting perceived gender is often dependent on its alignment with resonance characteristics (FFs).

Here is a detailed discussion of how $\text{F}_0$ and formant frequencies jointly affect gender perception:

### 1. $\text{F}_0$ as the Source, Formants as the Filter

$\text{F}_0$ is the acoustic measurement reflecting the number of vocal fold vibrations per second, which primarily determines the **pitch** of the voice. Formant frequencies are acoustic components amplified through **resonance** in the vocal tract, which is the filter that modifies the laryngeal tone produced by the vocal folds.

*   **Gender Association:** Higher $\text{F}_0$ values and higher FFs are associated with feminine perception, reflecting the smaller size of the female vocal folds ($\text{F}_0$) and vocal tract (FFs).
*   **Joint Contribution:** The perception of gender in voice is influenced by **both formant frequencies and $\text{F}_0$, acting in conjunction with one another**. The best-fitting models predicting perceived femininity included not only the main effects of FFs (specifically F2) and $\text{F}_0$, but also the **interaction between them**, suggesting a complex relationship.

### 2. The Dominance of Formants in Ambiguous Pitch Ranges

The interaction between $\text{F}_0$ and FFs becomes especially crucial when $\text{F}_0$ is in the **gender ambiguous or gender neutral range**. This range is typically defined around $\mathbf{145 \text{ Hz} \text{ to } 185\text{ Hz}}$.

*   **Resonance Overrides Pitch:** When $\text{F}_0$ is within the ambiguous range, the resonant characteristics can dictate the perceived gender.
*   **The Conflict Problem:** If $\text{F}_0$ is raised to a gender-ambiguous range ($\mathbf{150 \text{ Hz} \text{ to } 185\text{ Hz}}$), the voice of transgender women may **nevertheless often be perceived as that of cisgender men when the formant frequencies are still in the male area**. Listeners are aware when a speaker's $\text{F}_0$ and FFs conflict.
*   **FFs as the Salient Cue in Specific Contexts:** In connected speech, FFs are the **most salient cues** for gender perception, especially when $\text{F}_0$ is in the gender-ambiguous range. A study focusing on vowels with a constant gender-ambiguous $\text{F}_0$ of $175\text{ Hz}$ found that listeners' perception shifted to **"not-male"** only when FFs were manipulated.

### 3. Necessity of Joint Modification

Research shows that shifting only one parameter is generally insufficient to reliably change gender perception, demonstrating the necessity of jointly addressing both $\text{F}_0$ and FFs.

*   **Shifting Both is Highly Effective:** When **both** $\text{F}_0$ and formant frequencies (or the formant envelope) were shifted to values appropriate for the opposite gender, listeners identified the target gender approximately **$82\%$ of the time**. Manipulating both parameters effectively changes gender perception in running speech.
*   **Shifting Only $\text{F}_0$:** When $\text{F}_0$ only was shifted to the opposite gender's normative values, the perceived gender shift was less successful, occurring in about **$34\%$** of cases for male speakers. Raising $\text{F}_0$ alone has been found to be **insufficient** for consistently achieving increased feminine perception.
*   **Shifting Only FFs:** When formant frequencies only were shifted, the gender shift was even less marked, occurring in about **$19\%$** of cases for male speakers.

### 4. Specific Formant Contributions

Not all formants contribute equally to gender perception, and their interaction with $\text{F}_0$ is often focused on the lower formants:

*   **Second Formant ($\mathbf{F}2$):** $\text{F}2$ has been shown to correlate **most saliently** with the perception of gender. Higher $\text{F}2$ values were associated with higher ratings of perceived femininity. Targeting the shifting of **both $\text{F}_0$ and the second formant frequency ($\text{F}2$) is the most effective treatment method** in changing the perception of gender.
*   **First Formant ($\mathbf{F}1$):** In trans men, $\text{F}1$ was identified as an acoustic predictor of vocal masculinity, with lower $\text{F}1$ values being associated with masculine perception. Vowels characterized by a **high $\text{F}1$** frequency (such as /ɑ/ and /æ/) were **more readily perceived as "not-male"** than vowels with low $\text{F}1$ (such as /i/ and /u/) when $\text{F}_0$ was held constant at an ambiguous level.

In summary, the joint influence of $\text{F}_0$ and FFs means that they function as **redundant but necessary gender information**. If $\text{F}_0$ provides the coarse information necessary to establish a possible gender range, FFs provide the fine-tuning of the vocal tract filter that confirms the voice's perceived masculinity or femininity, preventing the listener from perceiving the voice as having an incongruent "source" and "filter".

The joint relationship between **Fundamental Frequency ($\text{F}_0$)** (pitch) and **Formant Frequencies (FFs)** (vocal tract resonance) is central and critically important to successful gender perception and affirmation. While $\text{F}_0$ is the most salient individual acoustic cue, its effectiveness in gender attribution relies heavily on being correctly matched with appropriate formant frequencies.

Here is a discussion of how $\text{F}_0$ and FFs jointly affect gender perception, encompassing the critical concepts of necessary modification, the "mismatch problem," and their relative contributions.

***

## 1. The Requirement for Joint Modification

Listeners use both $\text{F}_0$ and formant frequency information to identify a speaker’s gender. Research consistently demonstrates that manipulating **both parameters in concert** is significantly more effective at altering perceived gender than shifting either one alone.

*   **Maximum Gender Shift:** Studies where both $\text{F}_0$ and formant frequencies were shifted toward the values of the opposite gender resulted in listeners identifying the target (shifted) gender approximately **82% of the time**.
*   **Insufficiency of Single Cue:** When **only $\text{F}_0$ was shifted**, well under half of productions (e.g., 34.3% of male samples shifted to female $\text{F}_0$) were identified with the target gender. Similarly, shifting **only formant frequencies** was even less effective, with gender shift being much less marked (e.g., male speakers perceived as female about 19% of the time).
*   **Most Effective Treatment Method:** For transgender voice modification, focusing on the shifting of **both $\text{F}_0$ and the Second Formant Frequency ($\text{F}_2$)** is considered the **most effective treatment method** in changing the perception of gender.

## 2. The Interaction: Source-Filter Mismatch (The "Unnatural" Voice)

The combined effect of $\text{F}_0$ and FFs is understood through the **Source-Filter Theory** of voice production: $\text{F}_0$ is the source (vocal fold vibration), and formants (FFs) are the filter (vocal tract shape). The human vocal system is typically physically linked, so a lower $\text{F}_0$ naturally co-occurs with lower formants (larger vocal tract), and vice versa. Voice training can decouple these parameters, but this can lead to perceptual issues.

*   **The Mismatch Problem:** If a user raises their $\text{F}_0$ (pitch) without simultaneously "**brightening**" their resonance (raising their formants), the resulting sound (High $\text{F}_0$ + Low Formants) can be perceived as **"unnatural"** or "mismatched".
*   **Feminization Pitfall:** Raising $\text{F}_0$ alone is **frequently insufficient** for changing the auditory perception of vocal gender from male to female. Some transgender women find that, despite adopting a higher $\text{F}_0$, their voices continue to be perceived as male.
*   **Post-Surgical Mismatch:** After pitch-raising surgery, the fundamental frequency abruptly increases, but if the old phonatory pattern for resonance remains the same, a **mismatch** occurs between the elevated $\text{F}_0$ and the old formant frequencies. This can lead to the perceived pitch being lower.

## 3. The Decisive Role in the Gender Ambiguous Range

The joint effect of $\text{F}_0$ and FFs becomes most critical when the pitch falls within the overlap zone, forcing the listener to rely more heavily on resonance cues for attribution.

*   **Gender Ambiguous Zone:** The range of $\text{F}_0$ between approximately **150 Hz and 185 Hz** is considered the "gender ambiguous" or neutral range.
*   **Formants Tip the Scales:** If the $\text{F}_0$ is maintained within this gender ambiguous zone (e.g., at **175 Hz**), the voice of transgender women is **nevertheless often perceived as that of cisgender men when the formant frequencies are still in the male area**.
*   **Research Confirmation:** Studies manipulating vowels at a constant gender-ambiguous $\text{F}_0$ of 175 Hz confirmed that shifting FFs increased the perception of voice femininity beyond that of raising $\text{F}_0$ alone. **Resonance** (Formant Frequencies) can, in fact, sometimes influence gender perception **even more than pitch**.

## 4. Specific Formant Contributions to Femininity

The primary goal of feminization is to produce higher $\text{F}_0$ alongside higher FFs, which are associated with a smaller vocal tract and "bright" resonance.

*   **Predictors of Femininity:** Mean $\text{F}_0$ and **average formant frequency** (e.g., of the vowel /i/) are key acoustic predictors of perceived masculinity–femininity ratings.
*   **The Second Formant ($\text{F}_2$):** $\text{F}_2$ is widely cited as the **most critical formant** influencing gender perception, correlating roughly with the length of the oral cavity. Higher $\text{F}_2$ values (and thus higher $\text{F}_0$) are associated with **higher perceived femininity**.
*   **The First Formant ($\text{F}_1$):** $\text{F}_1$ is also important; voices are more readily perceived as "not-male" for vowels characterized by a naturally **high $\text{F}_1$ frequency**. For instance, vowels produced with high $\text{F}_1$ and $\text{F}_2$ frequencies, such as /had/ and /hid/ or /ɑ/ and /æ/, were rated as significantly more feminine than vowels with lower formants.

In sum, while $\text{F}_0$ provides the basic **pitch level** that dictates the binary assignment of a voice, FFs provide the **timbre and quality** that confirm whether that high or low pitch sounds natural for the affirmed gender. For successful gender affirmation, particularly feminization, $\text{F}_0$ and FFs must be **matched and moved together** toward higher frequencies to avoid the listener perception of an "unnatural" voice.


Explain the link between vocal tract size and formant frequencies.

The link between **vocal tract size** and **formant frequencies (FFs)** is an **inverse relationship** rooted in the physics of sound resonance: the physical dimensions of the vocal tract act as a filter that determines the frequencies at which sound energy is concentrated, or amplified.

Here is a detailed explanation of this link:

### The Vocal Tract as a Resonating Filter

The vocal tract, which consists of the throat (pharynx), mouth, and nose, functions as a resonating tube or filter for the raw sound produced by the vocal folds ($\text{F}_0$).

*   **Resonance Defined:** Resonance is the **amplification and filtering** of the complex laryngeal tone by the cavities of the vocal tract. This acoustic phenomenon is what gives the voice its unique tone or **timbre**.
*   **Formants Defined:** Formant frequencies are the **regions of energy concentration** in the acoustic signal, created by the amplification and damping of harmonics within the laryngeal spectrum as sound waves move through the vocal tract. Formants signal the identity of the vowel being produced.
*   **Modifiable Filter:** Unlike fixed instruments like a saxophone, the human vocal tract is unique because its **size and shape can change** through muscle movements, which in turn changes the resonant frequencies (formants).

### The Inverse Relationship

The size and shape of the resonating cavities in the vocal tract directly determine the frequency of the formants.

| Vocal Tract Dimension | Effect on Resonating Cavity | Resulting Formant Frequencies | Perceived Sound Quality |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Larger/Longer** Vocal Tract | Longer resonating spaces, larger cavities. | **Lower Formant Frequencies**. | **Darker** sound, often perceived as lower in pitch. |
| **Smaller/Shorter** Vocal Tract | Shorter resonating spaces, smaller cavities. | **Higher Formant Frequencies**. | **Brighter** sound, often perceived as higher in pitch.

In simplest terms, the **smaller the resonating space, the higher the vibrating frequencies** of the formants, and vice versa. This is analogous to a water glass, where the resonant frequency changes depending on the amount of water (or air space) inside.

### The Role of Vocal Tract Size in Gender Perception

The anatomical difference in vocal tract length between cisgender men and women is a major basis for observed formant differences and gender perception.

*   **Cisgender Differences:** Cisgender men generally have a **longer and larger** vocal tract compared to cisgender women, which amplifies lower frequencies. Consequently, average formant frequency values for cisgender women are approximately **$20\%$ higher** than those of cisgender men.
*   **Puberty Effects:** The anatomical differences often emerge during a testosterone-driven puberty, where the larynx grows larger, potentially contributing to a **larger and longer vocal tract**.
*   **Clinical Goal:** In voice feminization training, a key strategy is to achieve a perceptually smaller vocal tract by modifying articulatory behaviors (e.g., lip spreading, anterior tongue carriage) to **raise the frequency of the vowel formants overall**. This modification is often described as **changing the resonance to sound physically smaller** than the speaker is.

### Specific Formant Sensitivity to Vocal Tract Shape

The distinct shape of different sections of the vocal tract determines the characteristics of the individual formants.

*   **First Formant ($\text{F}1$):** This formant is primarily affected by the **size of the pharyngeal cavity** (the throat, from the glottis to the point of maximum tongue constriction).
    *   $\text{F}1$ is **inversely related to vowel height**. If the tongue position lowers (e.g., opening the jaw), $\text{F}1$ increases.
*   **Second Formant ($\text{F}2$):** This formant is chiefly determined by the **size of the oral cavity** (from the point of maximum constriction of the tongue to the lips).
    *   A **more forward tongue position** induces a higher $\text{F}2$.
