How tightly the vocal folds come together in phonation, often referred to as **adduction and abduction**, determines where the voice falls on the spectrum from **buzzy** (heavy) to **breathy** (light), with a **balanced** sound in the middle.

| Adduction/Closure Degree | Resulting Sound Quality | Mechanism/Acoustic Effect |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **Tight Adduction (Complete Closure)** | **Buzzy, Thicker, or Heavier Sound** | When vocal folds are pressed together **tightly**, the sound is buzzy, thicker, or heavier. This is often the target for a **masculine-sounding voice**. A **percussive attack** (hard onset or glottal attack) creates more vocal fold pressure and a heavier sound. |
| **Complete Adduction with Bubbling Air** | **Glottal Fry/Vocal Fry** | Glottal fry happens when the vocal folds **completely adduct** and then air is bubbled through them, creating a creaky, crackling sound. This sound is often associated with **rougher** or **groggier** qualities. |
| **Gentle/Minimal Adduction (Optimal Closure)** | **Balanced or Light Sound** | Phonation with **"just enough pressure"** yields a **balanced sound** that is neither buzzy nor breathy. Achieving a light sound requires **very gentle minimal adduction**. |
| **Over-Relaxed/Incomplete Adduction (Abduction)** | **Breathy or Airy Sound** | If the vocal folds are **over-relaxed** and **abducted** (moved apart) too much, air passes through, resulting in a **breathy** or **airy sound**. A slightly breathy onset can contribute to a perception of reduced speaking intensity and a slight increase in perceived femininity. |

### 2. Effect on Volume and Air Pressure

Vocal fold adduction is intrinsically linked to **loudness** because of the air pressure required for sound production:

*   **Loud Sounds:** Loud sounds require a **lot of air pressure** (subglottal pressure). To resist this higher pressure and maintain phonation, the vocal folds **have to adduct more tightly**, which creates a **thicker, buzzier, heavier sound**.
*   **Soft Sounds:** When speaking softly, the vocal folds press together **gently**, resulting in a **lighter vocal weight**.

It is important to note that everything related to vocal weight and adduction discussed above typically only applies at an **average speaking volume**; when shouting, the mechanics and qualities change.

### 3. Impact on Vocal Health and Efficiency

The degree and manner of adduction are critical for vocal health and efficiency:

*   **Hard Glottal Attacks (Abrupt Onset/Hard Adduction):** Hard phonation onset (HPO) is characterized by the **abrupt medial compression** of vocal folds before exhalation begins, which results in pre-phonatory **high subglottal pressure**. HPO is clinically considered **phono-traumatic** and has been found at higher frequencies in patients with voice disorders.
*   **Soft Phonation Onset (Gentle Adduction):** Soft phonation onset (SPO), characterized by the **simultaneous activity of exhaling and adduction** of the vocal folds, is considered the **least phono-traumatic and thus optimal initiation of phonation**.
*   **Breathy Voice:** A breathy voice, resulting from incomplete adduction, is generally considered **inefficient** because it **uses significantly more air** than a non-breathy voice.

In summary, the extent of vocal fold adduction is a critical factor in shaping voice quality, dictating the spectrum from a whispery, airy sound (less adduction/more abduction) to a thick, buzzy sound (tighter adduction).
Vocal fold **adduction** (coming together/closure) is the process by which the vocal folds approximate each other, acting as a valve within the larynx. This action is fundamental to voice production and profoundly affects the resulting sound's **quality**, **loudness**, **pitch**, and **onset**.

Here is how vocal fold adduction affects sound, broken down by specific acoustic outcomes:

### 1. Influence on Vocal Quality (Clarity vs. Breathiness)

The manner and efficiency of vocal fold adduction determine the overall quality of the voice, particularly across the spectrum of **breathy, modal, and pressed (or hard/clear) phonation**.

*   **Breathy Voice (Incomplete/Gentle Adduction):** When the vocal folds approach medial compression but are not entirely adducted, or if there is incomplete closure, air leaks through. This results in a sound characterized by **aperiodic (noise) energy**.
    *   **Perceptual Effect:** This creates a **breathy** or **whispery/light** voice quality. Increased breathiness is a cue sometimes associated with **feminine-sounding voices**.
    *   **Acoustic Correlate:** Breathiness results in **lower Harmonics-to-Noise Ratio (HNR)** values (below 8 dB suggest significant breathiness).
    *   **Open Quotient:** A higher **open quotient** (the proportion of the cycle the folds are open) corresponds to a **gentler, softer, or more breathy sound**.

*   **Pressed/Clear/Buzzy Voice (Firm Adduction):** This quality results from **thick, firm vocal fold closure**.
    *   **Perceptual Effect:** This quality is described as **brassier, heavier**, or clear. Tighter vocal fold adduction creates a **tense tone**.
    *   **Acoustic Correlate:** Clear, resonant voices have higher HNR values (above 12–14 dB).
    *   **Open Quotient:** A **lower open quotient** corresponds to a **more pressed or harsh sound**.

### 2. Influence on Vocal Onset

Vocal fold adduction governs how a sound begins, categorized into three types of phonation onset:

*   **Hard Phonation Onset (HPO) / Glottal Attack:** Characterized by the **abrupt medial compression of vocal folds before exhalation** is initiated (prior to transglottal air-stream). The folds come together **completely**, building air pressure that is suddenly released as they start vibrating. HPO is clinically considered undesirable, or phonotraumatic. Hard glottal attacks are commonly observed in clinical assessments.
*   **Soft Phonation Onset (SPO) / Gentle Onset:** Characterized by the **simultaneous activity of exhaling and adduction** of the vocal folds. This results in a **smooth and gradual approximation** of the vocal folds. SPO is considered the **least phonotraumatic and optimal initiation** of phonation. A smooth onset helps contribute to a perception of **reduced speaking intensity** and may **feminize** the voice.
*   **Breathy Phonation Onset:** Occurs when **exhalation and transglottal airflow happen before vocal fold adduction**. This is an exploration of onset where **breath is released before the vocal folds start vibrating**.

### 3. Influence on Aerodynamic Forces and Vibration

Adduction is the critical variable linking respiratory power (airflow/pressure) to acoustic output, governed by aerodynamic principles:

*   **Phonation:** During phonation (vocalization), the vocal folds **adduct** (come together), and air is blown through them, causing them to **vibrate and produce sound**.
*   **Air Pressure Build-Up:** Adduction allows the vocal folds to **act as a valve**, enabling the speaker to **build air pressure** for effortful tasks, such as lifting a heavy box.
*   **Glottal Closure Efficiency (Vocal Economy):** The degree of closure affects vocal economy. Exercises (like SOVTEs) that promote glottal closure lead to improved vocal efficiency and less effort required to maintain vibration. When back-pressure is created during semi-occluded phonation, it helps the vocal folds vibrate more easily, training them to adopt a pattern of **gentler, more efficient closure**.
*   **Hindered vs. Aided Vibration:** Excessive supraglottic pressure (which can result from increased resistance above the vocal folds) can increase the intraglottic pressure, **hindering the contact** between the vocal folds and leading to a **lower contact quotient (CQ)** and incomplete closure. Conversely, conditions that increase inertive reactance (such as during SOVTEs) aid vocal fold vibration and lead to **better defined stages of the vocal cycle with longer closed phases** (higher CQ).
